the professor by aka charlotte bronte currer bell preface
this little book be write before either jane eyre or shirley and yet no indulgence can be solicit for it on the plea of a first attempt
a first attempt it certainly be not as the pen which write it have be previously wear a good deal in a practice of some year
i have not indeed publish anything before i commence the professor but in many a crude effort destroy almost as soon as compose i have get over any such taste as i might once have have for ornamented and redundant composition and come to prefer what be plain and homely
at the same time i have adopt a set of principle on the subject of incident c such as would be generally approve in theory but the result of which when carry out into practice often procure for an author more surprise than pleasure
i say to myself that my hero should work his way through life as i have see real live man work theirs--that he should never get a shilling he have not earn--that no sudden turn should lift he in a moment to wealth and high station that whatever small competency he might gain should be win by the sweat of his brow that before he could find so much as an arbour to sit down in he should master at least half the ascent of the hill of difficulty that he should not even marry a beautiful girl or a lady of rank
as adam s son he should share adam s doom and drain throughout life a mixed and moderate cup of enjoyment
in the sequel however i find that publisher in general scarcely approve of this system but would have like something more imaginative and poetical--something more consonant with a highly wrought fancy with a taste for pathos with sentiment more tender elevated unworldly
indeed until an author have try to dispose of a manuscript of this kind he can never know what store of romance and sensibility lie hide in breast he would not have suspect of casket such treasure
man in business be usually think to prefer the real on trial the idea will be often find fallacious a passionate preference for the wild wonderful and thrilling--the strange startling and harrowing--agitate diver soul that show a calm and sober surface
such be the case the reader will comprehend that to have reach he in the form of a print book this brief narrative must have go through some struggle--which indeed it have
and after all its bad struggle and strong ordeal be yet to come but it take comfort--subdue fear--lean on the staff of a moderate expectation--and mutter under its breath while lift its eye to that of the public he that be low need fear no fall currer bell
the foregoing preface be write by my wife with a view to the publication of the professor shortly after the appearance of shirley be dissuade from her intention the authoress make some use of the material in a subsequent work-- villette as however these two story be in most respect unlike it have be represent to i that i ought not to withhold the professor from the public
i have therefore consent to its publication

nicholls haworth parsonage september 22 nd 1856

introductory
the other day in look over my paper i find in my desk the follow copy of a letter send by i a year since to an old school acquaintance dear charles i think when you and i be at eton together we be neither of we what could be call popular character you be a sarcastic observant shrewd cold-blooded creature my own portrait i will not attempt to draw but i cannot recollect that it be a strikingly attractive one--can you
what animal magnetism draw thee and i together i know not certainly i never experience anything of the pylades and orestes sentiment for you and i have reason to believe that you on your part be equally free from all romantic regard to i
still out of school hour we walk and talk continually together when the theme of conversation be our companion or our master we understand each other and when i recur to some sentiment of affection some vague love of an excellent or beautiful object whether in animate or inanimate nature your sardonic coldness do not move i
i feel myself superior to that check then as i do now
it be a long time since i write to you and a still long time since i see you
chance to take up a newspaper of your county the other day my eye fall upon your name
i begin to think of old time to run over the event which have transpire since we separate and i sit down and commence this letter
what you have be do i know not but you shall hear if you choose to listen how the world have wag with i
first after leave eton i have an interview with my maternal uncle lord tynedale and the hon
john seacombe
they ask i if i would enter the church and my uncle the nobleman offer i the living of seacombe which be in his gift if i would then my other uncle mr
seacombe hint that when i become rector of seacombe-cum-scaife i might perhaps be allow to take as mistress of my house and head of my parish one of my six cousin his daughter all of whom i greatly dislike
i decline both the church and matrimony
a good clergyman be a good thing but i should have make a very bad one
as to the wife--oh how like a night-mare be the thought of be bind for life to one of my cousin
no doubt they be accomplish and pretty but not an accomplishment not a charm of theirs touch a chord in my bosom
to think of pass the winter evening by the parlour fire-side of seacombe rectory alone with one of they--for instance the large and well-model statue sarah--no i should be a bad husband under such circumstance as well as a bad clergyman
when i have decline my uncle offer they ask i what i intend to do i say i should reflect
they remind i that i have no fortune and no expectation of any and after a considerable pause lord tynedale demand sternly whether i have thought of follow my father s step and engage in trade now i have have no thought of the sort
i do not think that my turn of mind qualify i to make a good tradesman my taste my ambition do not lie in that way but such be the scorn express in lord tynedale s countenance as he pronounce the word trade--such the contemptuous sarcasm of his tone--that i be instantly decide
my father be but a name to i yet that name i do not like to hear mention with a sneer to my very face
i answer then with haste and warmth i cannot do well than follow in my father s step yes i will be a tradesman my uncle do not remonstrate they and i part with mutual disgust
in review this transaction i find that i be quite right to shake off the burden of tynedale s patronage but a fool to offer my shoulder instantly for the reception of another burden--one which might be more intolerable and which certainly be yet untried
i write instantly to edward--you know edward--my only brother ten year my senior married to a rich mill-owner s daughter and now possessor of the mill and business which be my father s before he fail
you be aware that my father-once reckon a croesus of wealth--become bankrupt a short time previous to his death and that my mother live in destitution for some six month after he unhelped by her aristocratical brother whom she have mortally offend by her union with crimsworth the----shire manufacturer
at the end of the six month she bring i into the world and then herself leave it without i should think much regret as it contain little hope or comfort for she
my father s relation take charge of edward as they do of i till i be nine year old
at that period it chance that the representation of an important borough in our county fall vacant mr
seacombe stand for it
my uncle crimsworth an astute mercantile man take the opportunity of write a fierce letter to the candidate state that if he and lord tynedale do not consent to do something towards the support of their sister s orphan child he would expose their relentless and malignant conduct towards that sister and do his good to turn the circumstance against mr
seacombe s election
that gentleman and lord t
know well enough that the crimsworths be an unscrupulous and determined race they know also that they have influence in the borough of x---- and make a virtue of necessity they consent to defray the expense of my education
i be send to eton where i remain ten year during which space of time edward and i never meet
he when he grow up enter into trade and pursue his calling with such diligence ability and success that now in his thirtieth year he be fast make a fortune
of this i be apprise by the occasional short letter i receive from he some three or four time a year which say letter never conclude without some expression of determined enmity against the house of seacombe and some reproach to i for living as he say on the bounty of that house
at first while still in boyhood i could not understand why as i have no parent i should not be indebted to my uncle tynedale and seacombe for my education but as i grow up and hear by degree of the persevere hostility the hatred till death evince by they against my father--of the suffering of my mother--of all the wrong in short of our house--then do i conceive shame of the dependence in which i live and form a resolution no more to take bread from hand which have refuse to minister to the necessity of my die mother
it be by these feeling i be influence when i refuse the rectory of seacombe and the union with one of my patrician cousin
an irreparable breach thus be effect between my uncle and myself i write to edward tell he what have occur and inform he of my intention to follow his step and be a tradesman
i ask moreover if he could give i employment
his answer express no approbation of my conduct but he say i might come down to shire if i like and he would see what could be do in the way of furnish i with work i repress all--even mental comment on his note--pack my trunk and carpet-bag and start for the north directly
after two day travel railroad be not then in existence i arrive one wet october afternoon in the town of x----
i have always understand that edward live in this town but on inquiry i find that it be only mr
crimsworth s mill and warehouse which be situate in the smoky atmosphere of bigben close his residence lie four mile out in the country
it be late in the evening when i alight at the gate of the habitation designate to i as my brother s
as i advance up the avenue i could see through the shade of twilight and the dark gloomy mist which deepen those shade that the house be large and the ground surround it sufficiently spacious
i pause a moment on the lawn in front and lean my back against a tall tree which rise in the centre i gaze with interest on the exterior of crimsworth hall
edward be rich think i to myself
i believe he to be do well--but i do not know he be master of a mansion like this cut short all marvel speculation conjecture c i advance to the front door and ring
a man-servant open it--i announce myself--he relieve i of my wet cloak and carpet-bag and usher i into a room furnish as a library where there be a bright fire and candle burn on the table he inform i that his master be not yet return from x----market but that he would certainly be at home in the course of half an hour
be leave to myself i take the stuff easy chair cover with red morocco which stand by the fireside and while my eye watch the flame dart from the glow coal and the cinder fall at interval on the hearth my mind busy itself in conjecture concern the meeting about to take place
amidst much that be doubtful in the subject of these conjecture there be one thing tolerably certain--i be in no danger of encounter severe disappointment from this the moderation of my expectation guarantee i
i anticipate no overflowings of fraternal tenderness edward s letter have always be such as to prevent the engendering or harbouring of delusion of this sort
still as i sit await his arrival i feel eager--very eager--i cannot tell you why my hand so utterly a stranger to the grasp of a kindre hand clench itself to repress the tremor with which impatience would fain have shake it
i think of my uncle and as i be engage in wonder whether edward s indifference would equal the cold disdain i have always experience from they i hear the avenue gate open wheel approach the house mr
crimsworth be arrive and after the lapse of some minute and a brief dialogue between himself and his servant in the hall his tread draw near the library door--that tread alone announce the master of the house
i still retain some confused recollection of edward as he be ten year ago--a tall wiry raw youth now as i rise from my seat and turn towards the library door i see a fine-look and powerful man light-complexion well-make and of athletic proportion the first glance make i aware of an air of promptitude and sharpness show as well in his movement as in his port his eye and the general expression of his face
he greet i with brevity and in the moment of shake hand scan i from head to foot he take his seat in the morocco cover arm-chair and motion i to another send
i expect you would have call at the counting-house in the close say he and his voice i notice have an abrupt accent probably habitual to he he speak also with a guttural northern tone which sound harsh in my ear accustom to the silvery utterance of the south
the landlord of the inn where the coach stop direct i here say i
i doubt at first the accuracy of his information not be aware that you have such a residence as this oh it be all right he reply only i be keep half an hour behind time wait for you--that be all
i think you must be come by the eight o clock coach i express regret that he have have to wait he make no answer but stir the fire as if to cover a movement of impatience then he scan i again
i feel an inward satisfaction that i have not in the first moment of meeting betray any warmth any enthusiasm that i have salute this man with a quiet and steady phlegm
have you quite break with tynedale and seacombe he ask hastily
i do not think i shall have any further communication with they my refusal of their proposal will i fancy operate as a barrier against all future intercourse why say he i may as well remind you at the very outset of our connection that no man can serve two master acquaintance with lord tynedale will be incompatible with assistance from i there be a kind of gratuitous menace in his eye as he look at i in finish this observation
feel no disposition to reply to he i content myself with an inward speculation on the difference which exist in the constitution of man s mind
i do not know what inference mr
crimsworth draw from my silence--whether he consider it a symptom of contumacity or an evidence of my be cow by his peremptory manner
after a long and hard stare at i he rise sharply from his seat
to-morrow say he i shall call your attention to some other point but now it be supper time and mrs
crimsworth be probably wait will you come he stride from the room and i follow
in cross the hall i wonder what mrs
crimsworth might be

have peruse the fair page of mrs

cease to take part in the banter conversation that flow on between mr
and mrs

the face i remember have please i as a boy but then i do not understand it now i know how rare that class of face be in the world and i appreciate keenly its thoughtful yet gentle expression
the serious grey eye possess for i a strong charm as do certain line in the feature indicative of most true and tender feeling

good-bye for the present william crimsworth to this letter i never get an answer before my old friend receive it he have accept a government appointment in one of the colony and be already on his way to the scene of his official labour
what have become of he since i know not
the leisure time i have at command and which i intend to employ for his private benefit i shall now dedicate to that of the public at large
my narrative be not exciting and above all not marvellous but it may interest some individual who have toil in the same vocation as myself will find in my experience frequent reflection of their own
the above letter will serve as an introduction

chapter ii
a fine october morning succeed to the foggy evening that have witness my first introduction to crimsworth hall
i be early up and walk in the large park-like meadow surround the house
the autumn sun rise over the shire hill disclose a pleasant country wood brown and mellow vary the field from which the harvest have be lately carry a river glide between the wood catch on its surface the somewhat cold gleam of the october sun and sky at frequent interval along the bank of the river tall cylindrical chimney almost like slender round tower indicate the factory which the tree half conceal here and there mansion similar to crimsworth hall occupy agreeable site on the hill-side the country wear on the whole a cheerful active fertile look
steam trade machinery have long banish from it all romance and seclusion
at a distance of five mile a valley opening between the low hill hold in its cup the great town of x----
a dense permanent vapour brood over this locality--there lie edward s concern i force my eye to scrutinize this prospect i force my mind to dwell on it for a time and when i find that it communicate no pleasurable emotion to my heart--that it stir in i none of the hope a man ought to feel when he see lay before he the scene of his life s career--i say to myself william you be a rebel against circumstance you be a fool and know not what you want you have choose trade and you shall be a tradesman
look i continue mentally-- look at the sooty smoke in that hollow and know that there be your post
there you cannot dream you cannot speculate and theorize--there you shall out and work thus self-school i return to the house
my brother be in the breakfast-room
i meet he collectedly--i could not meet he cheerfully he be stand on the rug his back to the fire--how much do i read in the expression of his eye as my glance encounter his when i advance to bid he good morning how much that be contradictory to my nature

it be well i have take a resolution to endure for a time or his manner would have go far to render insupportable the disgust i have just be endeavour to subdue

have i then force of mind to cope with he

chapter iii
i serve edward as his second clerk faithfully punctually diligently
what be give i to do i have the power and the determination to do well
mr

chapter xix
novelists should never allow themselves to weary of the study of real life
if they observe this duty conscientiously they would give we few picture chequer with vivid contrast of light and shade they would seldom elevate their hero and heroine to the height of rapture--still seldomer sink they to the depth of despair for if we rarely taste the fulness of joy in this life we yet more rarely savour the acrid bitterness of hopeless anguish unless indeed we have plunge like beast into sensual indulgence abuse strained stimulate again overstrain and at last destroy our faculty for enjoyment then truly we may find ourselves without support rob of hope
our agony be great and how can it end
we have break the spring of our power life must be all suffer--too feeble to conceive faith--death must be darkness--god spirit religion can have no place in our collapse mind where linger only hideous and pollute recollection of vice and time bring we on to the brink of the grave and dissolution fling we in--a rag eat through and through with disease wring together with pain stamp into the churchyard sod by the inexorable heel of despair

i never believe you could be so cruel--i can love you no more i have much ado to explain to he with a steady voice the stern necessity of the deed he still with that inconsolable and bitter accent which i cannot render but which pierce my heart repeated-- he might have be cure--you should have try--you should have burn the wound with a hot iron or cover it with caustic
you give no time and now it be too late--he be dead he sink fairly down on the senseless carcase i wait patiently a long while till his grief have somewhat exhaust he and then i lift he in my arm and carry he to his mother sure that she would comfort he well
she have witness the whole scene from a window she would not come out for fear of increase my difficulty by her emotion but she be ready now to receive he
she take he to her kind heart and on to her gentle lap console he but with her lip her eye her soft embrace for some time and then when his sob diminish tell he that yorke have feel no pain in dying and that if he have be leave to expire naturally his end would have be most horrible above all she tell he that i be not cruel for that idea seem to give exquisite pain to poor victor that it be my affection for yorke and he which have make i act so and that i be now almost heart-break to see he weep thus bitterly
victor would have be no true son of his father have these consideration these reason breathe in so low so sweet a tone--married to caress so benign so tender--to look so inspire with pity sympathy--produce no effect on he
they do produce an effect he grow calm rest his face on her shoulder and lie still in her arm
look up shortly he ask his mother to tell he over again what she have say about yorke have suffer no pain and my not be cruel the balmy word be repeat he again pillow his cheek on her breast and be again tranquil
some hour after he come to i in my library ask if i forgave he and desire to be reconcile
i draw the lad to my side and there i keep he a good while and have much talk with he in the course of which he disclose many point of feeling and think i approve of in my son
i find it be true few element of the good fellow or the fine fellow in he scant sparkle of the spirit which love to flash over the wine cup or which kindle the passion to a destroy fire but i see in the soil of his heart healthy and swell germ of compassion affection fidelity
i discover in the garden of his intellect a rich growth of wholesome principle--reason justice moral courage promise if not blight a fertile bearing
so i bestow on his large forehead and on his cheek--still pale with tear--a proud and content kiss and send he away comfort
yet i see he the next day lay on the mound under which yorke have be bury his face cover with his hand he be melancholy for some week and more than a year elapse before he would listen to any proposal of have another dog
victor learn fast
he must soon go to eton where i suspect his first year or two will be utter wretchedness to leave i his mother and his home will give his heart an agonized wrench then the fagging will not suit he--but emulation thirst after knowledge the glory of success will stir and reward he in time
meantime i feel in myself a strong repugnance to fix the hour which will uproot my sole olive branch and transplant it far from i and when i speak to frances on the subject i be hear with a kind of patient pain as though i allude to some fearful operation at which her nature shudder but from which she fortitude will not permit she to recoil
the step must however be take and it shall be for though frances will not make a milksop of her son she will accustom he to a style of treatment a forbearance a congenial tenderness he will meet with from none else
she see as i also see a something in victor s temper--a kind of electrical ardour and power--which emit now and then ominous spark hunsden call it his spirit and say it should not be curb
i call it the leaven of the offending adam and consider that it should be if not whipped out of he at least soundly discipline and that he will be cheap of any amount of either bodily or mental suffering which will ground he radically in the art of self-control
france give this something in her son s marked character no name but when it appear in the grinding of his tooth in the glittering of his eye in the fierce revolt of feel against disappointment mischance sudden sorrow or suppose injustice she fold he to her breast or take he to walk with she alone in the wood then she reason with he like any philosopher and to reason victor be ever accessible then she look at he with eye of love and by love victor can be infallibly subjugate but will reason or love be the weapon with which in future the world will meet his violence
oh no
for that flash in his black eye--for that cloud on his bony brow--for that compression of his statuesque lip the lad will some day get blow instead of blandishment--kick instead of kiss then for the fit of mute fury which will sicken his body and madden his soul then for the ordeal of merited and salutary suffering out of which he will come i trust a wiser and a well man
